2267 <section id="_385">

2268

2269 <h2>

2270Digital Rectal Exam</h2>

2271

2272 <div id="_section_385" class="pdq-sections">

2273<p id="_386" tabindex="-1">

2274Although digital rectal exam (DRE) has been used for many years, careful evaluation of this modality

2275has yet to take place. The examination is inexpensive, relatively noninvasive, and nonmorbid and can be taught to nonprofessional health workers; however, its effectiveness depends on the skill and experience of the examiner. The possible contribution of routine annual screening by rectal examination in reducing prostate cancer mortality remains to be determined. </p>

2276<p id="_442" tabindex="-1">

2277Several observational studies have examined process measures such as sensitivity and case-survival data,

2278but without appropriate controls and with no adjustment for lead-time and

2279length biases.[<a href="#cit/section_10.1">

22801</a>

2281,<a href="#cit/section_10.2">

22822</a>

2283]</p>

2284<p id="_387" tabindex="-1">

2285In 1984, one study reported on 811 unselected patients aged 50 to 80 years who underwent rectal examination and follow-up.[<a href="#cit/section_10.3">

22863</a>

2287] Of 43

2288patients with a palpable abnormality in the prostate, 38 agreed to undergo biopsy.

2289The positive predictive value (PPV) of a palpable nodule, i.e., prostate cancer on

2290biopsy, was 29% (11 of 38). Further evaluation revealed that 45% of the cases

2291were stage B, 36% were stage C, and 18% were stage D. More results from the

2292same investigators revealed a 25% PPV, with 68% of the

2293detected tumors clinically localized but only approximately 30% pathologically

2294localized after radical prostatectomy.[<a href="#cit/section_10.4">

22954</a>

2296] Some investigators reported a high proportion of clinically localized disease when prostate cancer

2297is detected by routine rectal examination,[<a href="#cit/section_10.5">

22985</a>

2299] while others reported that even

2300with annual rectal examination, only 20% of cases are localized at

2301diagnosis.[<a href="#cit/section_10.6">

23026</a>

2303] It has been reported that 25% of men

2304presenting with metastatic disease had a normal prostate examination.[<a href="#cit/section_10.7">

23057</a>

2306]

2307Another case-control study examining screening with both DRE and prostate-specific antigen (PSA) found a reduction in prostate cancer mortality that was not statistically significant (odds ratio [OR], 0.7; 95% confidence interval [CI], 0.46&#8211;1.1). Most men in this study were screened with DRE rather than PSA.[<a href="#cit/section_10.8">

23088</a>

2309] All four of these case-control studies are consistent with a reduction of 20% to 30% in prostate cancer mortality. Potential biases inherent in this study design, however, limit the ability to draw conclusions on the basis of this evidence alone.</p>

2310<p id="_388" tabindex="-1">

2311Since PSA assays became widely available in the late 1980s, DRE alone is rarely discussed as a screening modality. Several studies have found that DRE has a poor predictive value for

2312prostate cancer if PSA is at very low levels. In the European Study on

2313Screening for Prostate Cancer, it was found that if DRE is used only for a

2314PSA higher than 1.5 ng/mL (thus, no DRE is performed with PSA &lt;1.5 ng/mL), 29% of all

2315biopsies would be eliminated while maintaining a 95% prostate cancer detection

2316sensitivity. By applying DRE only for patients with a PSA higher than 2.0 ng/mL, the

2317biopsy rate would decrease by 36%, while sensitivity would drop to only 92%.[<a href="#cit/section_10.9">

23189</a>

2319]

2320A previous report from this same institution found DRE to have poor performance

2321characteristics. Among 10,523 men randomly assigned to screening, it was

2322reported that the overall prostate cancer detection rate using PSA, DRE, and

2323transrectal ultrasound was 4.5%, compared with only 2.5% if DRE alone was used. Among men

2324with a PSA lower than 3.0 ng/mL, the PPV of DRE was only

23254% to 11%.[<a href="#cit/section_10.10">

232610</a>

2327] Despite the poor performance of DRE, a retrospective

2328case-control study of men in Olmsted County, Minnesota, who died of prostate

2329cancer found that case patients were less likely to have undergone DRE during

2330the 10 years before diagnosis of prostate cancer (OR, 0.51; 95%

2331 CI, 0.31&#8211;0.84). These data suggested that screening DREs

2332may prevent 50% to 70% of deaths from prostate cancer.[<a href="#cit/section_10.11">

233311</a>

2334] Contrary to

2335these findings, results from a case-control study of 150 men who ultimately

2336died of prostate cancer were compared with 299 controls without disease. In this

2337different population, a similar number of cases and controls had undergone DRE

2338during the 10-year interval before prostate cancer diagnosis.[<a href="#cit/section_10.12">

233912</a>

2340] One

2341case-control study reported no statistically significant association between

2342routine screening with DRE and occurrence of metastatic

2343prostate cancer.[<a href="#cit/section_10.13">

234413</a>

2345]

2346The Prostate Cancer Prevention Trial requested that all men undergo prostate biopsy at study end to address ascertainment bias; the sensitivity of DRE for prostate cancer was 16.7%. The sensitivity increased to 21.3% in men receiving finasteride.[<a href="#cit/section_10.14">

234714</a>

2348]</p>

2413 <section id="_392">

2414

2415 <h2>

2416PCA3</h2>

2417

2418 <div id="_section_392" class="pdq-sections">

2419<p id="_393" tabindex="-1">

2420The <em class="gene-name">

2421PCA3</em>

2422 gene assay was approved by the U.S. Food and Drug Administration in early 2012, with the intended use to aid in the decision for repeat biopsy in men with a previous negative biopsy for an elevated prostate-specific antigen and for whom a repeat biopsy is being considered for a persistently elevated PSA. This test is performed on a urine sample collected after an attentive digital rectal exam (several strokes applied firmly to the prostate to the right and left prostatic lobes). Using a threshold value of 60, this test enhances the detection of prostate cancer while reducing the number of biopsies in men who are expected to ultimately have a negative biopsy.[<a href="#cit/section_11.1">

24231</a>

2424]</p>

2425<h6 do-not-show="toc">

2426References</h6>

2427<ol>

2428<li id="section_11.1">

2429PROGENSA&#174; PCA3 Assay - P100033. Silver Spring, Md: U.S. Food and Drug Administration, 2012. <a href="https://www.accessdata.fda.gov/cdrh_docs/pdf10/p100033b.pdf" title="https://www.accessdata.fda.gov/cdrh_docs/pdf10/p100033b.pdf">

2430Available online</a>

2431. Last accessed May 22, 2023.</li>

2432</ol>

2439 <section id="_394">

2440

2441 <h2>

2442Frequency of Screening</h2>

2443

2444 <div id="_section_394" class="pdq-sections">

2445<p id="_395" tabindex="-1">

2446The optimal frequency and age range for prostate-specific antigen (PSA) (and digital rectal exam) testing are

2447unknown.[<a href="#cit/section_12.1">

24481</a>

2449-<a href="#cit/section_12.3">

24503</a>

2451] Cancer detection rates have been reported to be similar for intervals of 1 to 4 years.[<a href="#cit/section_12.4">

24524</a>

2453] With serial annual screening in the Prostate, Lung, Colorectal, and Ovarian (PLCO) Cancer Screening Trial, 8% of men with baseline PSA lower than 1 ng/mL had a prostate cancer diagnosis within 2 years.[<a href="#cit/section_12.5">

24545</a>

2455] In the same trial, 2-year intervals in screening produced average delays of 5.4 to 6.5 months, while 4-year screening intervals produced average delays of 15.6 months (baseline PSA, &lt;1 ng/mL) to 20.9 months (baseline PSA, 3&#8211;4 ng/mL).[<a href="#cit/section_12.5">

24565</a>

2457] While the authors caution that an optimal prostate screening frequency cannot be determined from these data, they conclude that among men who choose to be screened, these data may provide a context for determining a PSA screening schedule.

2458</p>

2459<p id="_396" tabindex="-1">

2460A report from the European Randomized Study of Screening for Prostate Cancer (ERSPC) trial demonstrated that while more frequent screenings lead to more diagnosed cancers, the detection rate reported for aggressive interval cancers was very similar in the two countries despite their use of different screening frequencies (0.11 with a 4-year interval in Rotterdam and 0.12 with a 2-year interval in Gothenburg). The report suggests that mortality outcomes from the ERSPC (2- and 4-year intervals) and PLCO (1-year interval relative to opportunistic screening) trials should facilitate a more reliable assessment of the benefits and costs of different screening intervals.[<a href="#cit/section_12.6">

24616</a>

2462]</p>

2497 <section id="_397">

2498

2499 <h2>

2500Types of Tumors Detected by Prostate Cancer Screening</h2>

2501

2502 <div id="_section_397" class="pdq-sections">

2503<p id="_398" tabindex="-1">

2504Of serious concern regarding prostate cancer screening is the high

2505prevalence of histologically defined cancer. It has been demonstrated that

2506a considerable fraction (approximately one-third) of men in their fourth and

2507fifth decades have histologically evident prostate cancer.[<a href="#cit/section_13.1">

25081</a>

2509] Most of these

2510tumors are well-differentiated and microscopic in size. Conversely, evidence

2511suggests that tumors of potential clinical importance are larger and of higher

2512grade.[<a href="#cit/section_13.2">

25132</a>

2514] Since the inception of prostate-specific antigen (PSA) screening, the following events have occurred:

2515(1) a contemporaneous but unrelated decrease in detection of transition-zone

2516tumors, caused by a fall in the number of transurethral resections of the

2517prostate due to the advent of effective treatment for benign prostatic

2518hyperplasia (including alpha blockers and finasteride); and (2) an increase in

2519detection of peripheral-zone tumors due to the incorporation of transrectal ultrasound-guided prostate biopsies. Because transition-zone tumors are

2520predominantly low volume and low grade and because peripheral-zone tumors have a

2521preponderance of moderate-grade and high-grade disease, the proportion of higher-grade tumors detected by current screening practices has increased

2522substantially. A Detroit study found that between 1989 and 1996,

2523poorly differentiated tumors remained stable and well-differentiated tumors

2524fell in frequency while moderately differentiated disease increased in

2525frequency. The largest rise in incidence was in clinically localized

2526disease.[<a href="#cit/section_13.3">

25273</a>

2528] It is now known that systematic changes to the histological interpretation of biopsy specimens by anatomical pathologists has occurred during the PSA screening era (i.e., since about 1985) in the United States.[<a href="#cit/section_13.4">

25294</a>

2530] This phenomenon, sometimes called grade inflation, is the apparent increase in the distribution of high-grade tumors in the population over time but in the absence of a true biological or clinical change. It is possibly the result of an increasing tendency for pathologists to read tumor grade as more aggressive.[<a href="#cit/section_13.5">

25315</a>

2532]</p>

2533<p id="_399" tabindex="-1">

2534Prostate biopsies in a small percentage of men will demonstrate prostatic intraepithelial neoplasia (PIN). High-grade PIN is not cancer but may predict an increased risk of prostate cancer. PSA does not appear to be elevated with PIN.[<a href="#cit/section_13.6">

25356</a>

2536,<a href="#cit/section_13.7">

25377</a>

2538]</p>

2562<li id="section_13.6">

2563Lefkowitz GK, Sidhu GS, Torre P, et al.: Is repeat prostate biopsy for high-grade prostatic intraepithelial neoplasia necessary after routine 12-core sampling? Urology 58 (6): 999-1003, 2001.&#160;<a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&amp;db=PubMed&amp;list_uids=11744476&amp;dopt=Abstract" title="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&amp;db=PubMed&amp;list_uids=11744476&amp;dopt=Abstract">

2564[PUBMED Abstract]</a>

2565</li>

2566<li id="section_13.7">

2567O'Shaughnessy JA, Kelloff GJ, Gordon GB, et al.: Treatment and prevention of intraepithelial neoplasia: an important target for accelerated new agent development. Clin Cancer Res 8 (2): 314-46, 2002.&#160;<a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&amp;db=PubMed&amp;list_uids=11839647&amp;dopt=Abstract" title="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&amp;db=PubMed&amp;list_uids=11839647&amp;dopt=Abstract">

2568[PUBMED Abstract]</a>

2569</li>

2570</ol>

2571</div>

2572

2573 </section>

2574

2575

2576

2577 <section id="_402">

2578

2579 <h2>

2580Simulation Models</h2>

2581

2582 <div id="_section_402" class="pdq-sections">

2583<p id="_403" tabindex="-1">

2584Several computer simulation models have been developed to analyze trends in prostate cancer detection. The models were also developed to compare these trends with the reported decrease in prostate cancer deaths observed in the United States since the early 1990s, to investigate the cost-effectiveness of various screening strategies, and to attempt to estimate overdiagnosis resulting from screening.</p>

2585<p id="_439" tabindex="-1">

2586

2587

2588One of the first models looked at trends in prostate cancer detection compared with prostate cancer deaths between 1992 and 1994. Changes in prostate cancer mortality could not be explained entirely by prostate-specific antigen (PSA) screening alone.[<a href="#cit/section_14.1">

25891</a>

2590] Simulation modeling from the National Cancer Institute's Cancer Intervention and Surveillance Modeling Network (CISNET) program suggested that the combination of changes in prostate cancer treatment, improvements in disease management after primary therapy, and screening contributed to the drop in prostate cancer mortality.[<a href="#cit/section_14.2">

25912</a>

2592] CISNET models calibrated to Surveillance, Epidemiology, and End Results (SEER) Program incidence data were also used to estimate overdiagnosis caused by PSA screening in the United States, suggesting 23% to 42% of all screen-detected prostate cancers were overdiagnosed.[<a href="#cit/section_14.3">

25933</a>

2594] An analysis using the Microsimulation Screening Analysis (MISCAN) model and data from the European Randomized Study of Screening for Prostate Cancer trial predicted the numbers of prostate cancers diagnosed, the prostate cancer deaths averted, the quality-adjusted life years gained, and the cost-effectiveness of 68 screening strategies.[<a href="#cit/section_14.4">

25954</a>

2596]</p>

2597<p id="_440" tabindex="-1">

2598An example of the underlying assumptions and concerns about models is provided by a microsimulation modeling effort that examined the comparative effectiveness of 35 screening strategies, which varied by start and stop ages, screening intervals, and thresholds for biopsy referral.[<a href="#cit/section_14.5">

25995</a>

2600] The CISNET model assumes prostate cancer progression from onset to metastasis to clinical diagnosis in the absence of screening, with risks of events indicated by PSA levels. Event rates through the progression states are identified by matching model incidence to observed incidence, although it is not clear that the rates so identified are unique. Survival depends on stage at diagnosis, and screening is assumed to identify some cancers at an earlier stage than without screening, leading to a reduction in mortality. This <strong>

2601stage-shift</strong>

2602 model is virtually guaranteed to produce a benefit of screening.</p>

2603<h6 do-not-show="toc">

2604References</h6>

2605<ol>

2606<li id="section_14.1">

2607Etzioni R, Legler JM, Feuer EJ, et al.: Cancer surveillance series: interpreting trends in prostate cancer--part III: Quantifying the link between population prostate-specific antigen testing and recent declines in prostate cancer mortality. J Natl Cancer Inst 91 (12): 1033-9, 1999.&#160;<a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&amp;db=PubMed&amp;list_uids=10379966&amp;dopt=Abstract" title="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&amp;db=PubMed&amp;list_uids=10379966&amp;dopt=Abstract">

2608[PUBMED Abstract]</a>

2609</li>

2632

2633 <section id="_404">

2634

2635 <h2>

2636Providing Information to the Public, to Patients, and to Their Families</h2>

2637

2638 <div id="_section_404" class="pdq-sections">

2639<p id="_460" tabindex="-1">

2640While awaiting results of current studies, physicians and men (and their partners) are faced with the dilemma of whether to recommend or request a screening test. A qualitative study undertaken on focus groups of men, physician experts, and couples with screened and unscreened men has explored types of information that may help inform a man making a decision regarding prostate-specific antigen screening.[<a href="#cit/section_15.1">

26411</a>

2642] At a minimum, men should be informed about the possibility that false-positive or false-negative test results can occur, that it is not known whether regular screening will reduce the number of deaths from prostate cancer, and that among experts, the recommendation to screen is controversial.[<a href="#cit/section_15.2">

26432</a>

2644,<a href="#cit/section_15.3">

26453</a>

2646]</p>

2647<h6 do-not-show="toc">

2648References</h6>

2649<ol>

2650<li id="section_15.1">

2651Chan EC, Sulmasy DP: What should men know about prostate-specific antigen screening before giving informed consent? Am J Med 105 (4): 266-74, 1998.&#160;<a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&amp;db=PubMed&amp;list_uids=9809686&amp;dopt=Abstract" title="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&amp;db=PubMed&amp;list_uids=9809686&amp;dopt=Abstract">

2652[PUBMED Abstract]</a>

2653</li>

2654<li id="section_15.2">

2655O'Connor AM, Stacey D, Rovner D, et al.: Decision aids for people facing health treatment or screening decisions. Cochrane Database Syst Rev (3): CD001431, 2001.&#160;<a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&amp;db=PubMed&amp;list_uids=11686990&amp;dopt=Abstract" title="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&amp;db=PubMed&amp;list_uids=11686990&amp;dopt=Abstract">

2656[PUBMED Abstract]</a>

2657</li>

2658<li id="section_15.3">

2659Volk RJ, Hawley ST, Kneuper S, et al.: Trials of decision aids for prostate cancer screening: a systematic review. Am J Prev Med 33 (5): 428-434, 2007.&#160;<a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&amp;db=PubMed&amp;list_uids=17950409&amp;dopt=Abstract" title="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&amp;db=PubMed&amp;list_uids=17950409&amp;dopt=Abstract">

2660[PUBMED Abstract]</a>

2661</li>

2662</ol>

2663</div>

2664

2665 </section>

2666

2667

2668

2669 <section id="_443">

2670

2671 <h2>

2672Harms of Screening</h2>

2673

2674 <div id="_section_443" class="pdq-sections">

2675<p id="_444" tabindex="-1">

2676Screening increases the detection of indolent, unsuspected, and asymptomatic prostate cancer. Any potential benefits derived from screening asymptomatic men need to be weighed against the harms of screening and diagnostic procedures and treatments for prostate cancer. These harms are particularly burdensome to men with false-positive screening results and men who are unnecessarily treated because of overdiagnosis.</p>

2677<p id="_484" tabindex="-1">

2678 An unintended consequence of screening and biopsy is the erroneous assumption that a screened population is at increased risk of developing significant disease. In a study that examined the magnitude of prostate cancer risk associated with specific factors across the Selenium and Vitamin E Cancer Prevention Trial (SELECT) and Prostate Cancer Prevention Trial cohorts, the authors demonstrated that the likelihood of undergoing screening and biopsy depends on certain known or suspected risk factors. In turn, differential screening and biopsy can result in spurious conclusions regarding risk factors for prostate cancer.[<a href="#cit/section_16.1">

26791</a>

2680] For example, the authors explained that the labeling of a random characteristic such as blue eyes as a risk factor may increase biopsy rates among men with blue eyes, resulting in detection of indolent prostate cancer and leading to the inaccurate conclusion that blue eyes are a risk factor for prostate cancer.</p>

2681<p id="_486" tabindex="-1">

2682Negative impacts of screen detection on measures of risk may include the following:</p>

2683<div class="pdq-content-list">

2684<ul id="_487">

2685<li>

2686Interventions that may have no effect on prostate cancer course and may have harmful side effects.</li>

2687<li>

2688Time, cost, and anxiety associated with a diagnosis of inconsequential disease.</li>

2689<li>

2690Misdirection of research focus and resources.</li>

2691</ul>

2692</div>

2693<p id="_488" tabindex="-1">

2694Measurements of risk in men who undergo screening differ from measurements of risk in men who do not undergo screening. Past and current screening and biopsy practices may misrepresent prostate cancer risk factors. Better methods for identifying consequential prostate cancer are needed to avoid unnecessary biopsies.[<a href="#cit/section_16.1">

26951</a>

2696]</p>

2697<p id="_445" tabindex="-1">

2698Three cohort studies in Sweden and the United States linked databases to examine the association between a new diagnosis of prostate cancer and cardiovascular events/death or suicide. One Swedish study found that in the first year after a diagnosis of prostate cancer, the risk of death from cardiovascular disease (CVD) was increased in men diagnosed with prostate cancer compared with men who were not diagnosed with prostate cancer (relative risk [RR], 1.9; 95% confidence interval [CI], 1.9&#8211;2.0; adjusted for age, calendar period, and time since diagnosis). The risk of death from CVD was highest in the first week after diagnosis (RR, 11.2; 95% CI, 10.4&#8211;12.1) and was also higher in younger men (age &lt;54 years). These risks were lower in men diagnosed in the most recent time periods. Also, in the first year after diagnosis, the risk of committing suicide was higher for men who had been diagnosed with prostate cancer (RR, 2.6; 95% CI, 2.1&#8211;3.0; adjusted for age, calendar period, marital status, educational level, and history of psychiatric hospitalization). Again, this was highest in the first week after diagnosis (RR, 8.4; 95% CI, 1.9&#8211;22.7).[<a href="#cit/section_16.2">

26992</a>

2700] A second Swedish study largely confirmed these findings.[<a href="#cit/section_16.3">

27013</a>

2702]</p>

2703<p id="_446" tabindex="-1">

2704A U.S. cohort study explored the association between prostate cancer diagnosis and CVD mortality or suicide in men diagnosed with prostate cancer, compared with population-level expected rates during three different time periods (preprostate-specific antigen [pre-PSA], peri-PSA, and post-PSA). For CVD mortality, the standardized mortality ratio (SMR) was elevated for men diagnosed with prostate cancer in the first month after diagnosis in all time periods (overall SMR, 2.05; 95% CI, 1.89&#8211;2.22), but decreased in later months during the first year (decreasing to &lt;1.0 in the PSA time period). This association was not changed significantly by age, race, or tumor grade. SMRs were higher for nonmarried men, for men who lived in lower educational status or higher poverty counties, and for men with metastatic disease at diagnosis. Also, in the first 3 months after diagnosis, the SMR for suicide was higher in men with prostate cancer (SMR, 1.9; 95% CI, 1.4&#8211;2.6). In months 4 to 12, the SMR was lower but still greater than 1.0. The SMR for suicide, however, was greater than 1.0 only in the pre-PSA and peri-PSA time periods, but not in the post-PSA time period. SMR was higher for nonmarried men but did not vary by education or poverty.[<a href="#cit/section_16.4">

27054</a>

2706]</p>

2707<p id="_447" tabindex="-1">

2708These data lend credence to the concern that overdiagnosis of prostate cancer due to screening could lead to an increased risk of CVD mortality or suicide.</p>

2709<p id="_448" tabindex="-1">

2710Although there is no literature suggesting serious complications of digital rectal examination (DRE) or transrectal sonography, and the harms associated with venipuncture for PSA testing can be regarded as trivial, prostatic biopsies are associated with important complications. Transient fever, pain, hematospermia, and hematuria are all common, as are positive urine cultures.[<a href="#cit/section_16.5">

27115</a>

2712-<a href="#cit/section_16.7">

27137</a>

2714] Sepsis occurs in approximately 0.4% of men.[<a href="#cit/section_16.6">

27156</a>

2716,<a href="#cit/section_16.8">

27178</a>

2718]</p>

2719<p id="_449" tabindex="-1">

2720Long-term complications of radical prostatectomy include urinary incontinence, urethral stricture, erectile dysfunction, and the morbidity associated with general anesthesia and a major surgical procedure. Fecal incontinence can also occur. The associated mortality rate is reported to be 0.1% to 1%, depending on age. In the population-based Prostate Cancer Outcomes Study, 8.4% of 1,291 men were incontinent and 59.9% were impotent at 18 or 24 months following radical prostatectomy. More than 40% of men reported that their sexual performance was a moderate-to-large problem. Both sexual and urinary function varied by age, with younger men relatively less affected.[<a href="#cit/section_16.8">

27218</a>

2722,<a href="#cit/section_16.9">

27239</a>

2724]</p>

2725<p id="_450" tabindex="-1">

2726Definitive external-beam radiation therapy can result in acute cystitis, proctitis, and sometimes enteritis. These conditions are generally reversible but may be chronic. In the short-term, potency is preserved with irradiation in most cases but may diminish over time. A systematic review of evidence radiation therapy complications shows that 20% to 40% of men who had no erectile dysfunction before treatment developed dysfunction 12 to 24 months afterward. Furthermore, 2% to 16% of men who had no urinary incontinence before treatment developed dysfunction 12 to 24 months afterward, and about 18% of men had some bowel dysfunction 1 year after treatment. The magnitude of effects of brachytherapy has not been determined, but the spectrum of complications are similar.[<a href="#cit/section_16.10">

272710</a>

2728] Radiation to the prostate has been reported to increase the risk of secondary malignancies, most notably of the rectum and bladder. While the relative risk in a large Surveillance, Epidemiology and End Results (SEER)-based study was 1.26 (95% CI, 1.21&#8211;1.30), the absolute increase in risk is low. The same review of evidence found hormone therapy with luteinizing hormone-releasing hormone (LHRH) agonists reduces sexual function by 40% to 70%, and hormone therapy is associated with breast swelling in 5% to 25% of men. Hot flashes occur in 50% to 60% of men taking LHRH agonists.[<a href="#cit/section_16.8">

27298</a>

2730] For more information, see <a href="/types/prostate/hp/prostate-treatment-pdq">

2731Prostate Cancer Treatment</a>

2732.</p>

2733<p id="_451" tabindex="-1">

2734The question of whether prostate cancer treatment contributes to symptoms among screened prostate cancer survivors was addressed in an analysis from the Prostate, Lung, Colorectal and Ovarian (PLCO) Cancer Screening Trial. The

2735randomized controlled PLCO analysis compared 529 prostate cancer survivors, 5 to 10 years postdiagnosis, with 514 noncancer controls, regarding prostate cancer-specific symptomatology. There was poorer sexual and urinary function among prostate cancer survivors compared with noncancer controls, suggesting that these symptoms are related to prostate cancer treatment, not aging or comorbidities.[<a href="#cit/section_16.11">

273611</a>

2737]</p>

2738<p id="_452" tabindex="-1">

2739Screening has increased the incidence of prostate cancer. In the current medical climate, most early-stage prostate cancers are treated by radical surgery or irradiation with intent to eradicate the pathology. There is evidence that not all patients diagnosed with prostate cancer because of screening are in immediate need of curative treatment. Death from other causes often occurs before screen detected, localized, and well-differentiated malignancies affect the survival of these patients. To avoid overtreatment and consequent morbid events, active surveillance (AS) is an emerging strategy applicable in these kinds of cases wherein curative treatment is delayed pending objective medical evidence of disease progression.[<a href="#cit/section_16.12">

274012</a>

2741]</p>

2742<p id="_453" tabindex="-1">

2743The effectiveness of AS was investigated retrospectively in the European Randomized Study of Screening for Prostate Cancer (ERSPC) trial. Data from 577 men diagnosed with prostate cancer because of periodic screening between 1994 and 2007 at a mean age of 66.3 years in four participating clinical centers in the Netherlands, Sweden, and Finland were evaluated. Selection criteria for inclusion in the analysis were:</p>

2744<div class="pdq-content-list">

2745<ul id="_454">

2746<li>

2747PSA less than or equal to 10 ng/mL.</li>

2748<li>

2749PSA density less than 0.2 ng/mL.</li>

2750<li>

2751Stage T1C/T2.</li>

2752<li>

2753Gleason score less than or equal to 3 + 3 = 6.</li>

2754<li>

2755No more than two positive biopsy cores.</li>

2756</ul>

2757</div>

2758<p id="_455" tabindex="-1">

2759Men with positive lymph nodes or distant metastases at the time of diagnosis were excluded from the analysis. These are the same thresholds being applied in the (yet unreported) prospective Prostate Cancer Research International: Active Surveillance study on AS originating from ERSPC and in the (also unreported) protocol-based prospective study of AS in Canada.</p>

2760<p id="_456" tabindex="-1">

2761The mean follow-up time for the 577 men in the retrospective assessment was 4.35 years (0&#8211;11.63 years). The calculated 10-year prostate cancer-specific survival rate was 100%. The overall 10-year survival rate was 77%. The calculated 10-year deferred treatment-free survival rate was 43%.</p>

2762<p id="_457" tabindex="-1">

2763After 7.75 years, 50% of men had received treatment. The median treatment-free survival was 2.5 years. Men treated during follow-up were slightly younger at diagnosis than men remaining untreated (64.7 years vs. 67.0 years; <em>

2764P</em>

2765 &lt; .001). Of the 110 men shifting to active treatment despite favorable PSA levels and PSA doubling times, DRE was known in 53 of the men and played a role in nine of them, whereas rebiopsies were known in 27 of the men and played a role in none of them. On the basis of PSA characteristics, 1.9% of patients who remained untreated may have been better candidates for active treatment, while 55.8% of men who received active treatment were not obvious candidates for radical treatment, and neither DRE nor rebiopsy explained the discrepancy. Factors like anxiety and urologic complaints may have been more explanatory, but the data were not available.</p>

2766<p id="_458" tabindex="-1">

2767The authors concluded that their data confirmed previous studies' findings, that many screen-detected prostate cancers may be actively followed (e.g., AS), and curative treatment delayed, thereby delaying or avoiding the morbid consequences of radical therapy without diminishing survival. The authors also noted that a considerable fraction of men do not comply with the AS regimen, apparently for psychological reasons, and AS often resulted in delay, not avoidance, of radical therapy.</p>

2768<p id="_463" tabindex="-1">

2769In the Prostate Testing for Cancer and Treatment (ProtecT) study, 1,643 men with localized prostate cancer were randomly assigned equally to active monitoring, surgery, or radiation therapy. The primary end point was death from prostate cancer, and secondary outcomes were clinical (local) progression, metastases, and death from all causes.[<a href="#cit/section_16.13">

277013</a>

2771]</p>

2772<p id="_465" tabindex="-1">

2773In a substudy of ProtecT that examined patient-reported outcomes, the response rate was over 85% for most of the questionnaires used to examine quality of life. The study addressed urinary, bowel, and sexual function and specific effects on quality of life, anxiety and depression, and general health. No methods were employed to deal with nonresponse or missing responses. In a quality-of-life study, nonresponse tends to be informative, so this lapse is unusual.[<a href="#cit/section_16.14">

277414</a>

2775]</p>

2776<p id="_466" tabindex="-1">

2777Results showed that men who had undergone prostatectomy reported more impotence and incontinence; men who received radiation reported more bowel dysfunction; and men who received active monitoring reported the lowest levels of these adverse effects. In general, differences decreased over the 6 years that data were collected. Overall, mental and physical health did not differ by treatment.[<a href="#cit/section_16.14">

277814</a>

2779]</p>

2780<p id="_459" tabindex="-1">

2781Whatever the screening modality, the screening process itself can lead to psychological effects in men who have a prostate biopsy but do not have prostate cancer. One study of these men at 12 months after their negative biopsy who reported worrying that they may develop cancer (<em>

2782P</em>

2783 &lt; .001), showed large increases in prostate-cancer worry compared with men with a normal PSA (26% vs. 6%).[<a href="#cit/section_16.15">

278415</a>

2785] In the same study, biopsied men were more likely than those in the normal PSA group to have had at least one follow-up PSA test in the first year (73% vs. 42%; <em>

2786P</em>

2787 &lt; .001), more likely to have had another biopsy (15% vs. 1%; <em>

2788P</em>

2789 &lt; .001), and more likely to have visited a urologist (71% vs. 13%; <em>

2790P</em>

2791 &lt; .001).</p>

2809[PUBMED Abstract]</a>

2810</li>

2811<li id="section_16.5">

2812Aus G, Ahlgren G, Bergdahl S, et al.: Infection after transrectal core biopsies of the prostate--risk factors and antibiotic prophylaxis. 2862 <section id="_80">

2863

2864 <h2>

2865Changes to This Summary (05/22/2023)</h2>

2866

2867 <div id="_section_80" class="pdq-sections">

2868<p id="_82" tabindex="-1">

2869The PDQ cancer information summaries are reviewed regularly and updated as

2870new information becomes available. This section describes the latest

2871changes made to this summary as of the date above.</p>

2872<p id="_597" tabindex="-1">

2873<strong>

2874<a href="/types/prostate/hp/prostate-screening-pdq#_1">

2875Overview</a>

2876</strong>

2877</p>

2878<p id="_598" tabindex="-1">

2879Added <a href="/types/prostate/hp/prostate-screening-pdq#_2">

2880text</a>

2881 to state that the Overview section summarizes the published evidence on the topic of prostate cancer screening. The rest of the summary describes the evidence in more detail.</p>

2882<p id="_590" tabindex="-1">

2883<strong>

2884<a href="/types/prostate/hp/prostate-screening-pdq#_429">

2885Incidence and Mortality of Prostate Cancer</a>

2886</strong>

2887</p>

2888<p id="_591" tabindex="-1">

2889Updated <a href="/types/prostate/hp/prostate-screening-pdq#_430">

2890statistics</a>

2891 with estimated new cases and deaths for 2023 (cited American Cancer Society as reference 1). Also revised text to state that between the mid-1990s and mid-2010s, mortality rates declined by about 50%; however, from 2016 to 2020, mortality rates decreased by 0.6% per year. The estimated lifetime risk of a prostate cancer diagnosis is about 12.6%, and the lifetime risk of dying from this disease is 2.3%.</p>

2892<p id="_592" tabindex="-1">

2893Revised <a href="/types/prostate/hp/prostate-screening-pdq#_431">

2894text</a>

2895 to state that cancer statistics from the National Cancer Institute indicated that between 2013 and 2019 the proportion of disease diagnosed at a locoregional stage was 82%, and the proportion of disease diagnosed as distant disease was 8%.</p>

2896<p id="_disclaimerHP_3" tabindex="-1">

2897This summary is written and maintained by the <a href="https://www.cancer.gov/publications/pdq/editorial-boards/screening-prevention" title="https://www.cancer.gov/publications/pdq/editorial-boards/screening-prevention">

2898PDQ Screening and Prevention Editorial Board</a>

2899, which is

2900editorially independent of NCI. The summary reflects an independent review of

2901the literature and does not represent a policy statement of NCI or NIH. More

2902information about summary policies and the role of the PDQ Editorial Boards in

2903maintaining the PDQ summaries can be found on the <a href="/types/prostate/hp/prostate-screening-pdq#_AboutThis_1">

2904About This PDQ Summary</a>

2905 and <a href="https://www.cancer.gov/publications/pdq" title="https://www.cancer.gov/publications/pdq">

2906PDQ&#174; Cancer Information for Health Professionals</a>

2907 pages.

2908</p>

2909</div>

2910

2911 </section>

2914

2915 <section id="_AboutThis_1">

2916

2917 <h2>

2918About This PDQ Summary</h2>

2919

2920 <div id="_section_AboutThis_1" class="pdq-sections">

2921<section id="_AboutThis_2">

2922<h3 id="_AboutThis_2_toc">

2923Purpose of This Summary</h3>

2924<p id="_AboutThis_3" tabindex="-1">

2925This PDQ cancer information summary for health professionals provides comprehensive, peer-reviewed, evidence-based information about prostate cancer screening. It is intended as a resource to inform and assist clinicians in the care of their patients. It does not provide formal guidelines or recommendations for making health care decisions.</p>

2926</section>

2927<section id="_AboutThis_4">

2928<h3 id="_AboutThis_4_toc">

2929Reviewers and Updates</h3>

2930<p id="_AboutThis_5" tabindex="-1">

2931This summary is reviewed regularly and updated as necessary by the <a href="https://www.cancer.gov/publications/pdq/editorial-boards/screening-prevention" title="https://www.cancer.gov/publications/pdq/editorial-boards/screening-prevention">

2932PDQ Screening and Prevention Editorial Board</a>

2933, which is editorially independent of the National Cancer Institute (NCI). The summary reflects an independent review of the literature and does not represent a policy statement of NCI or the National Institutes of Health (NIH).</p>

2934<p id="_AboutThis_22" tabindex="-1">

2935 Board members review recently published articles each month to determine whether an article should:</p>

2936<div class="pdq-content-list">

2937<ul id="_AboutThis_6">

2938<li>

2939be discussed at a meeting,</li>

2940<li>

2941be cited with text, or</li>

2942<li>

2943replace or update an existing article that is already cited.</li>

2944</ul>

2945</div>

2946<p id="_AboutThis_7" tabindex="-1">

2947Changes to the summaries are made through a consensus process in which Board members evaluate the strength of the evidence in the published articles and determine how the article should be included in the summary.</p>

2948<p id="_AboutThis_9" tabindex="-1">

2949Any comments or questions about the summary content should be submitted to Cancer.gov through the NCI website's <a href="https://www.cancer.gov/contact/email-us" title="https://www.cancer.gov/contact/email-us">

2950Email Us</a>

2951. Do not contact the individual Board Members with questions or comments about the summaries. Board members will not respond to individual inquiries.</p>

2952</section>

2953<section id="_AboutThis_10">

2954<h3 id="_AboutThis_10_toc">

2955Levels of Evidence</h3>

2956<p id="_AboutThis_11" tabindex="-1">

2957Some of the reference citations in this summary are accompanied by a level-of-evidence designation. These designations are intended to help readers assess the strength of the evidence supporting the use of specific interventions or approaches. The PDQ Screening and Prevention Editorial Board uses a <a href="/publications/pdq/levels-evidence/screening-prevention">

2958formal evidence ranking system</a>

2959 in developing its level-of-evidence designations.</p>

2960</section>

2961<section id="_AboutThis_12">

2962<h3 id="_AboutThis_12_toc">

2963Permission to Use This Summary</h3>

2964<p id="_AboutThis_13" tabindex="-1">

2965PDQ is a registered trademark. Although the content of PDQ documents can be used freely as text, it cannot be identified as an NCI PDQ cancer information summary unless it is presented in its entirety and is regularly updated. However, an author would be permitted to write a sentence such as &#8220;NCI&#8217;s PDQ cancer information summary about breast cancer prevention states the risks succinctly: [include excerpt from the summary].&#8221;</p>

2966<p id="_AboutThis_14" tabindex="-1">

2967The preferred citation for this PDQ summary is:</p>

2968<p id="_AboutThis_15" tabindex="-1">

2969PDQ&#174; Screening and Prevention Editorial Board. PDQ Prostate Cancer Screening. Bethesda, MD: National Cancer Institute. Updated &lt;MM/DD/YYYY&gt;. Available at: <a href="https://www.cancer.gov/types/prostate/hp/prostate-screening-pdq" title="https://www.cancer.gov/types/prostate/hp/prostate-screening-pdq">

2970https://www.cancer.gov/types/prostate/hp/prostate-screening-pdq</a>

2971. Accessed &lt;MM/DD/YYYY&gt;. [PMID: 26389383]</p>

2972<p id="_AboutThis_16" tabindex="-1">

2973Images in this summary are used with permission of the author(s), artist, and/or publisher for use within the PDQ summaries only. Permission to use images outside the context of PDQ information must be obtained from the owner(s) and cannot be granted by the National Cancer Institute. Information about using the illustrations in this summary, along with many other cancer-related images, is available in <a href="https://visualsonline.cancer.gov/" title="https://visualsonline.cancer.gov/">

2974Visuals Online</a>

2975, a collection of over 2,000 scientific images.

2976

2977</p>

2978</section>

2980<h3 id="_AboutThis_17_toc">

2981Disclaimer</h3>

2982<p id="_AboutThis_19" tabindex="-1">

2983The information in these summaries should not be used as a basis for insurance reimbursement determinations. More information on insurance coverage is available on Cancer.gov on the <a href="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/managing-care" title="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/managing-care">

2984Managing Cancer Care</a>

2985 page.</p>

2986</section>
